⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذا رفع رأسه من السجدة الثانية في الركعة الثانية افترش رجله اليسرى فجلس ... حنفیہ کے نزدیک دونوں تشہد میں دائیں پاؤں کو کھڑا کرے گا اور بائیں پاؤں کو بچھا کر اس پر بیٹھے گا۔ فقال ابو حميد الساعدى فاذا جلس في الركعتين جلس على رجله اليسرى و نصب اليمني واذا جلس في الركعة الآخرة قدم رجله اليسرى ونصب الاخرى وقعد على مقعدته (د) ( بخاری شریف، باب سنۃ الجلوس في التشهد ص ۱۱۴ حاشیہ : (الف) جابر بن سمرۃ فرماتے ہیں کہ حضور ہمارے سامنے آئے اور فرمایا کہ کیا بات ہے کہ اپنے ہاتھوں کو اٹھائے ہوئے ہوں ۔ گویا کہ دوڑنے والے گھوڑے کی دم ہوں۔ نماز میں سکون سے رہو (ب) فرمایا میں نے نماز پڑھی نبی اور حضرت ابو بکر اور حضرت عمر کے ساتھ ، پس انہوں نے ہاتھ نہیں اٹھائے مگر صرف تکبیر اولی کے وقت نماز کے شروع میں (ج) ابن عمر جب نماز میں داخل ہوتے ... جب دونوں رکعتوں سے کھڑے ہوتے تو ہاتھ اٹھاتے ۔ ابن عمر نے اس حدیث کو حضور تیک مرفوع کیا (1) ابوحمید ساعدی نے فرمایا۔ پس جب دور کھتوں پر بیٹھے تو بائیں پاؤں پر بیٹھے اور دائیں پاؤس کو کھڑا کرے۔ پس جب آخری رکعت (باقی اگلے صفحہ پر ) ... (ص ۱۵۱) ... (۲۰۴) (۳۴) ووجّه اصابعه نحو القبلة و وضع يديه على ... نبر ۸۲۸ مسلم شریف، باب ما تجمع صفة الصلوة وما شت به ص ۱۹۴ نمبر ۴۹۸) (۲) عن وائـل بـن حـجـر قــال قـدمــت المدينة قلت لانظرن الى صلوة رسول الله الله فلما جلس يعنى للتشهد افترش رجله اليسرى ووضع يده اليسرى يعنى على فخذه اليسرى ونصب رجله اليمنى (الف) ( ترندی شریف، باب کیف الجلوس في التشهد ص ۶۵ نمبر ۲۹۲) ان احادیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ تشہد میں دائیں پاؤں کو کھڑا رکھنا چاہئے اور بائیں پاؤں کو بچھا کر اس پر بیٹھنا چاہئے ۔ اور دوسری حدیث میں قعدہ اولی اور قعدہ اخیرہ کی بھی قید نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے دونوں قعدہ میں حنفیہ کے نزدیک ایک طرح بیٹھنا مسنون ہے۔ ا امام مالک کے نزدیک آخری تشہد میں تو رک مسنون ہے۔ تو رک کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دونوں پاؤں پیچھے کر دے اور مقعد پر بیٹھے ۔ ان کی دلیل بخاری والی اوپر کی حدیث ہے ( بخاری شریف نمبر (۸۲۸) (۲) حدیث میں ہے حتی اذا كانت السجدة التي فيها التسليم اخر رجله اليسرى وقعد متوركا على شقه الايسر (ب) ( مسلم شریف ، باب صفۃ الجلوس في الصلوة وكيفية وضع اليدين على الفخذین (۲۱۶ نمبر ۷۹ ۵ را بو داؤد شریف ، باب من ذكر التورک فی الرابعۃ ص ۴۵ انمبر ۹۶۳) اس حدیث سے ثابت ہوا کہ قعدہ اخیرہ میں تورک مسنون ہے۔
So when he raises his head from the second prostration in the second rak'ah, he spreads his left foot and sits on it... According to the Hanafis, in both tashahhuds he will keep the right foot upright and spread the left foot and sit on it. Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi said: 'When he sits in the two rak'ahs (first tashahhud), he sits on his left foot and erects the right; and when he sits in the last rak'ah, he brings forward his left foot, erects the other, and sits on his buttocks' (Bukhari). From these hadiths it is known that in tashahhud the right foot should be kept upright and the left foot spread and sat upon. And there is no restriction of first or last sitting in the second hadith. Therefore, according to the Hanafis, the same sitting is sunnah in both sittings. Imam Malik holds that in the final tashahhud, tawarruk is sunnah. Tawarruk means to push both feet back and sit on the buttocks. His proof is the above hadith from Bukhari (no. 828) and the hadith: 'When it was the prostration in which there was the taslim, he moved back his left foot and sat mutawarrikan on his left side' (Muslim, Abu Dawud). From this hadith it is proven that tawarruk in the final sitting is sunnah.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding the sitting posture in tashahhud. The Hanafi position is based on the practice of the Prophet as reported by Wail ibn Hujr and Abu Humayd: in both sittings, whether first or final, the worshipper sits on the left foot (iftrash) with the right foot upright. The Maliki school distinguishes: in the final tashahhud, one should sit mutawarrikan (tawarruk), leaning on the left buttock with both feet pushed out to the right. Their proof is the hadith from Muslim describing the Prophet's final sitting. The Hanafis respond that the hadith of Abu Humayd indicates that the Prophet did iftrash in the first sitting and tawarruk in the last, but the hadith of Wail ibn Hujr does not restrict it, so the stronger view is to do iftrash in both. The passage thereby establishes the Hanafi ruling: the same sitting is sunnah in both tashahhuds.

Key Terms
افتراش (iftrāsh): Sitting on the left foot spread out, with the right foot upright.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the buttocks with both feet pushed out to the right side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the sunnah sitting posture in the final tashahhud?
A) Tawarruk (sitting on buttocks)
B) Iftirash (sitting on left foot with right foot upright)
C) Sitting cross-legged
D) Kneeling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah is to sit iftrash (on left foot) in both tashahhuds; tawarruk is a valid alternative from the Maliki school but not preferred in Hanafi fiqh.

Salah — Prayer

Finger placement and direction in tashahhud
Ruling on foot and hand posture during tashahhud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah postures of the feet and hands during tashahhud according to the Hanafi school and cite the hadith evidence for each.

Core Idea
Point toes to qibla, make a ring and point index finger — sunnah from explicit hadith.

The tashahhud is not only about words; the physical posture is a sunnah that the Prophet ﷺ demonstrated. In this lesson, you will learn the specific positions of the feet and hands during tashahhud according to the Hanafi school, supported by authentic hadiths. Understanding these postures will help you follow the sunnah precisely in your daily prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key actions
What are the two main parts of the tashahhud posture described? (Feet and hands). List the specific actions: right foot vertical, left foot spread, hands on thighs, right hand making a ring and pointing with index finger.
These are all sunnah, not fard.
2
Find the hadith for foot position
Locate the hadith from Ibn Umar that states it is from the sunnah to direct the toes of the right foot toward the qibla. Understand that this proves the action is sunnah.
3
Find the hadith for hand position
Locate the hadith from Wail ibn Hujr that describes the Prophet making a ring with thumb and middle finger and pointing with the index finger. Note the words 'halaqa halqah' and 'ashara bil-sabbabah'.
The hadith also mentions placing hands on thighs.
4
Understand the ruling level
These actions are sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah). Omitting them does not invalidate the prayer but loses reward. Differentiate from wajib or fard.
The Hanafi school considers these as sunnah, not wajib.
5
Note the Hanafi distinctive practice
The Hanafi school insists on continuous pointing of the index finger from the beginning of tashahhud, not only at the Shahadah. Also, the ring is made throughout.
Some differences exist among other schools.

الاستقبال با طراف اصابع القدم القبلة عند القعود التشهد ص ۱۶۱، نمبر ۱۱۵۹) (۲) واقبل بصدرة اليمنى على قبلته (ابوداؤد شریف، باب من ذکر التورک فی الرابعہ نمبر ۷ ۹۶ اس باب کی آخری حدیث ہے ) اس حدیث سے بن حـجـر قـال ... ثم جلس فافترش رجله اليسرى ووضع يده اليسرى على فخذه اليسرى وحد مرفقه الايمن على فخذه اليمنى وقبض ثنتين و حلق حلقة ورأيته يقول هكذا وحلق بشر الابهام والوسطى واشار بالسبابة (و)(ابوداؤد شریف ، باب کیف الجلوس في التشهد ص ۴۵ نمبر ۹۵۷) عـاصـم بـن كـلـيـب الجرمي عن ابيه عن جده قال دخلت على النبي حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) پر بیٹھے تو بایاں پاؤں آگے کرے اور دائیں پاؤں کو کھڑا کرے اور اپنی مقعد پر بیٹھے (یعنی تو رک کرے ) ( الف ) وائل بن حجر فرماتے ہیں کہ میں مدینہ آیا تا کہ حضور کی نماز دیکھوں۔ پس جہ کہ تشہد کے لئے بیٹھے تو بائیں پاؤں کو بچھایا اور اس پر بائیں ہاتھ کو رکھا یعنی بائیں ران پر اور دائیں پاؤں کو کھڑا کیا (ب) یہاں تک کہ جب وہ سجدہ ہوا جس میں سلام کرنا ہے تو بائیں پاؤں کو پیچھے کیا اور بائیں جانب میں تو رک کر کے بیٹھے ( ج ) عبداللہ بن عمر فرماتے ہیں کہ نماز کی سنت میں سے یہ ہے کہ دائیں قدم کو کھڑا کرے اور اپنی انگلیوں سے قبلے کا استقبال کرے اور بائیں پاؤں پر بیٹھے ( د) وائل بن حجر فرماتے ہیں کہ ... پھر بیٹھے اور اپنا بایاں پاؤں بچھایا اور بایاں ہاتھ بائیں ران پر رکھا اور دائیں کمپنی کو حد کی طرح بنایا اور خنصر اور چھوٹی انگلیوں کو موڑا اور پورا حلقہ بنایا اور میں نے دیکھا کہ وہ اس طرح کر رہے تھے ۔ راوی بشر نے انگوٹھے اور درمیان کی انگلیوں سے حلقہ بنایا اور شہادت کی انگلی سے اشارہ کیا۔
And he directs his toes toward the qibla and places his hands on his thighs and spreads his fingers... The proof for directing toes toward qibla is the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar: 'It is from the sunnah of prayer to erect the right foot, facing the qibla with its toes, and to sit on the left' (Nasa'i). And another hadith: 'He faced the front of his right foot toward his qibla' (Abu Dawud). From these hadiths it is known that when sitting in tashahhud, the toes should be directed toward the qibla. The proof for placing fingers on thighs and making a ring with the right hand and pointing is in the hadith of Wail ibn Hujr: '...then he sat, spread his left foot, placed his left hand on his left thigh, placed his right elbow on his right thigh, clenched two fingers, made a ring, and I saw him saying thus, and Bishr made a ring with thumb and middle finger and pointed with the index finger' (Abu Dawud).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage provides the rulings on the detailed posture of the hands and feet during tashahhud. The author states that the toes of the right foot should be directed toward the qibla, and the hands should be placed on the thighs with fingers spread. Additionally, the right hand should form a ring by joining the thumb and middle finger, while pointing with the index finger. These actions are all proven from the sunnah through specific hadiths: the hadith of Ibn Umar for the toes, and the hadith of Wail ibn Hujr for the hand gestures. The passage thus establishes the complete physical posture of the tashahhud according to the Hanafi school, emphasizing that these are sunnah actions that complete the prayer.

Key Terms
السبابة (al-sabbābah): The index finger used for pointing during tashahhud.
حلق حلقة (halaqa halqah): To make a ring by joining the thumb and middle finger, while pointing with the index finger.

A) Both feet flat on the ground
B) Right foot vertical with toes pointing to qibla, left foot spread
C) Both feet vertical
D) Sitting on both feet

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah for tashahhud includes pointing the right foot's toes toward the qibla and making a ring with the right hand while pointing with the index finger, all proven from authentic hadiths.

Sitting Posture and Hand Gestures in Tashahhud: Hanafi vs Maliki
Ruling on sitting and hand gestures in tashahhud with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting posture during tashahhud and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Maliki school regarding iftirash and tawarruk.

Core Idea
Iftirash (sitting on the left foot) applies to both tashahhuds in the Hanafi school, based on general hadith, while Maliki restricts tawarruk to the final sitting.

The posture a worshipper adopts during the tashahhud is a matter of sunnah, but the schools differ on which posture applies to which sitting. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position that iftirash (spreading the left foot and sitting on it) is the sunnah for both the first and final tashahhud. We will contrast this with the Maliki view that tawarruk (sitting on the buttocks with feet to the right) is specific to the final sitting, and understand the textual basis for each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts in question
What are the specific actions of sitting and hand placement during tashahhud? Locate the parts of the passage that describe foot posture, hand placement on thighs, and finger gestures.
Focus on the iftirash and tawarruk descriptions.
2
State the Hanafi position with its dalil
What is the iftirash posture? Which hadith (Wail bin Hujr and Ibn Umar) support it? Note that the Hanafi school applies it to both sittings because the hadith do not specify a particular sitting.
The hadith from Tirmidhi and Nasa'i are key.
3
Present the Maliki counter-position
What is tawarruk? Which hadith (Bukhari and Muslim) does the Maliki school cite for the final sitting? Understand their argument that the final sitting is distinct.
The hadith mentions 'the prostration containing the salutation'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school reconcile the conflicting hadith? Why do they consider the Maliki hadith specific to the final sajdah and their own hadith general?
The Hanafi principle: a general command applies to all instances unless a specific exception is proven.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in prayer? How would a Hanafi worshipper sit compared to a Maliki? Consider also the hand gestures described.
The hand gestures (ring and pointing) are agreed upon.

(Part of ruling on sitting) And he erects the right foot and directs his toes towards the Qibla and places his hands on his thighs. From the hadith of Wail bin Hujr: when he sat for tashahhud, he spread his left foot and placed his left hand on his left thigh and erected his right foot. These hadith show that in tashahhud the right foot should be erect and the left foot should be spread and one sits on it. Hanafis consider this sitting in both sittings. Imam Malik considers tawarruk in the final tashahhud: sitting with both feet to the right on the buttocks. His proof: the hadith in Bukhari: 'until when he performed the prostration containing the salutation, he placed his left foot behind and sat in tawarruk on his left side.' Then the ruling: direct toes towards Qibla, place hands on thighs with fingers spread. Proof: hadith of Ibn Umar: 'it is from the sunnah of prayer to erect the right foot and face its toes towards the Qibla and sit on the left foot.' And hadith of Wail: 'then he sat and spread his left foot, placed his left hand on his left thigh, and placed his right forearm on his right thigh, then grasped two fingers and made a ring, and I saw him pointing with the index finger.' The footnote: the hadith of Asim bin Kulayb from his father from his grandfather: 'I entered upon the Prophet while he was praying, his left hand on his left thigh and right hand on his right thigh, with fingers folded except the index finger.'
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sitting posture (qa'dah) during tashahhud in prayer, along with hand gestures, and includes an inter-madhhab dispute between Hanafi and Maliki. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position from the matn: the right foot is erected with toes facing the Qibla, hands are placed on the thighs. Then he provides the proof from the hadith of Wail bin Hujr (reported in Tirmidhi and others) showing iftirash: spreading the left foot and sitting on it while erecting the right foot. The author notes that since no distinction is made between the first and final sitting, Hanafis apply iftirash in both. Then he presents the Maliki view: tawarruk (sitting on the buttocks with feet to the right) is sunnah in the final tashahhud, citing the hadith in Bukhari and Muslim about the Prophet tawarruk in the last sajdah before salutation. The Hanafi response is implicit: the Maliki hadith is specific to the final sitting, while the Hanafi hadith is general. Next, the text returns to the matn: directing toes to the Qibla and placing hands on thighs with fingers spread, supported by the hadith of Ibn Umar. The detailed hadith of Wail bin Hujr also describes the hand gesture: making a ring with the thumb and middle finger and pointing with the index finger. The footnote adds the hadith of Asim bin Kulayb, confirming the hand posture. Thus, the complete Hanafi practice for the sitting is: iftirash (left foot spread, right foot erect), right hand makes a ring (thumb and middle finger) and points the index finger, left hand rests on left thigh with fingers spread.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The sitting in prayer to recite the testimony of faith (al-tahiyyat).
الافتراش (al-iftirash): To spread the left foot and sit on it while erecting the right foot.
التورك (al-tawarruk): To sit on the buttocks with both feet placed towards the right side, left foot behind.
السبابة (al-sababah): The index finger used for pointing during tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the correct sitting posture during the final tashahhud?
A) Tawarruk (sitting on buttocks with feet to the right)
B) Iftirash (sitting on left foot with right foot erect)
C) Kneeling on both knees
D) Sitting cross-legged

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school adopts iftirash for both tashahhuds because the hadith evidence is general; the Maliki restriction to tawarruk in the final sitting is considered a specific exception by them, but Hanafis maintain the general practice.

Preferred Wording of Tashahhud: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on preferred tashahhud wording with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi preferred tashahhud, identify the Hanafi dalil from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i position, including the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The Hanafi tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is preferred because its hadith contains an explicit command (amr) indicating wujub.

The tashahhud is a wajib part of the prayer, but the exact wording varies between schools. This lesson examines why the Hanafi school prefers the tashahhud of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud over that of Ibn Abbas, as favoured by the Shafi'is. We will analyse the proofs from the Sunnah and understand the practical difference in recitation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact wording of tashahhud that the Hanafi school prefers (Ibn Mas'ud's version) and that it is considered afdal (preferred).
The full text begins with 'التحيات لله والصلوات والطیبات...'
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognise that the Shafi'i school prefers the tashahhud of Ibn Abbas, which begins with 'التحيات المباركات...'.
Both schools claim the Prophet taught the tashahhud like a Quranic surah.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith of Ibn Abbas where the Prophet taught 'التحيات المباركات...'.
The hadith is in Muslim and Tirmidhi.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand why the Hanafi school argues that Ibn Mas'ud's hadith is stronger: it includes the explicit command 'فليقل' (let him say), which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation, and the Prophet personally taught Ibn Mas'ud the tashahhud as if teaching a surah.
The command elevates the status of Ibn Mas'ud's narration over Ibn Abbas's.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis recite Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud, while Shafi'is recite Ibn Abbas's version with additional blessings on the Prophet's family.
This difference does not affect the validity of prayer; both are acceptable.

Then he recites the tashahhud, and the tashahhud is to say: al-tahiyyatu lillahi wa'l-salawatu wa'l-tayyibatu, al-salamu 'alayka ayyuha al-nabiyyu wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuhu, al-salamu 'alayna wa 'ala 'ibadillahi al-salihin, ashhadu an la ilaha illallah, wa ashhadu anna Muhammadan 'abduhu wa rasuluhu. Then he says: Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammadin wa 'ala ali Muhammadin kama sallayta 'ala Ibrahima wa 'ala ali Ibrahima innaka hamidun majid, Allahumma barik 'ala Muhammadin wa 'ala ali Muhammadin kama barakta 'ala Ibrahima wa 'ala ali Ibrahima innaka hamidun majid. Then he says the salutation to the right and left: al-salamu 'alaykum wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuhu. This is the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud, and for the Hanafis it is preferable to recite it. The reason is that Abdullah ibn Mas'ud was commanded to recite this tashahhud. He said: We used to say while praying behind the Prophet: 'Peace be upon Jibril and Mika'il, peace be upon so-and-so.' The Prophet turned to us and said: 'Indeed Allah is the source of peace, so when one of you prays, let him say: al-tahiyyatu lillahi...' This hadith contains the command 'فليقل' which indicates obligation, so Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud is better with us. Also, in Muslim's hadith: 'The Prophet taught me the tashahhud as he taught me a surah of the Qur'an.' This gives it more importance. Imam Shafi'i considers the tashahhud of Ibn Abbas better. His proof: the hadith of Ibn Abbas: 'The Prophet used to teach us the tashahhud as he taught us a surah of the Qur'an, and he would say: al-tahiyyatu al-mubarakatu...'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the preferred wording of the tashahhud in prayer, with an inter-madhhab dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i. The author begins with the matn: the tashahhud is to recite the well-known formula beginning with 'التحيات لله...' then the salawat (blessings on the Prophet) and the salutation (taslim). The author notes that this is the tashahhud of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. For Hanafis, this wording is preferable (afdal). The proof is the hadith in Bukhari and Muslim: when the companions used to say 'Peace be upon Jibril and Mika'il', the Prophet corrected them, saying 'Allah is the peace' and commanded with the word 'فليقل' (let him say) the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud. The command 'فليقل' indicates wujub (obligation) in Hanafi usul, so this tashahhud is not just recommended but carries weight. Furthermore, the Prophet taught Ibn Mas'ud the tashahhud directly, holding his hand as if teaching a Quranic surah (Muslim). This elevates its status. Then the author presents the Shafi'i position: they prefer the tashahhud of Ibn Abbas, which begins with 'التحيات المباركات...' Their proof is the hadith where the Prophet taught them the tashahhud like a Quranic surah, and he said 'التحيات المباركات...' (Muslim). The author implies but does not explicitly state the Hanafi response: the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud includes the explicit command, and the teaching method is similar; the Hanafi preference is based on the stronger textual evidence (the command and the specific teaching). Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation in the sitting position of prayer that begins with 'التحيات لله' and includes the testimony of faith.
التحيات (al-tahiyyat): The first word of the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud, meaning 'all greetings' or 'all salutations'.
أمر (amr): A command form in Arabic grammar; here 'فليقل' is an imperative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because Ibn Mas'ud's version is shorter
D) Because the majority of scholars agree

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud because of the explicit command in the hadith, making it the mufta bihi position.

Hand Placement and Finger Gesture in Tashahhud
Sunnah act of hand placement during tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi sunnah hand placement during tashahhud and state the correct finger gesture for the index finger.

Core Idea
The index finger points at the Shahada; the right hand forms a circle with clenched fingers.

Tashahhud is a wajib part of the prayer, but the specific hand positions and finger gestures are sunnah (confirmed practice). This lesson describes how the Hanafi school holds the hands and fingers during tashahhud, based on a hadith from Tirmidhi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah act
Locate the ruling on hand placement during tashahhud. The author states that the left hand is placed on the left thigh, and the right hand on the right thigh. This is a sunnah act.
The tashahhud itself is wajib, but the hand position is sunnah.
2
Detail the right hand gesture
The right hand fingers are clenched (qabd) except for the index finger, which is extended (bast al-sababa). This forms a circle-like grip.
The clenched fingers symbolize humility, the raised finger signifies Tawheed.
3
Note the timing of pointing
The index finger is raised specifically when saying 'la ilaha illallah' (the Shahada). It is not pointed throughout the entire tashahhud.
The gesture is made during the testimony of faith.

The Urdu commentary adds that this gesture is specifically when saying 'la ilaha illallah'. This is a confirmed Sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) in the Hanafi school, and the pointing with the index finger symbolizes the oneness of Allah.

بَسْط السَّبَّابَة (bast al-sababa): Extending the index finger while keeping other fingers clenched, for pointing during tashahhud

Tashahhud Wording and Its Obligation (Hanafi vs Shafi'i)
States the preferred tashahhud wording and ruling of obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the preferred tashahhud wording, explain the proof from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The command 'falyaqul' makes Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud the preferred wording in Hanafi prayer, and tashahhud itself is wajib.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on the tashahhud recitation during prayer. The school holds that tashahhud is wajib (obligatory) and that the wording taught by the Prophet to Abdullah ibn Mas'ud is the most virtuous. This view is based on an explicit command and the teaching method. We will contrast this with the Shafi'i position, which prefers a different wording taught to Ibn Abbas.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: tashahhud is wajib, and Ibn Mas'ud's wording is preferred.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: the imperative 'falyaqul' indicates obligation, and the teaching like a surah emphasizes its importance.
Focus on the command form (amr) as the key proof.
3
State the opposing Shafi'i position
Identify that the Shafi'i school prefers Ibn Abbas's wording and considers tashahhud sunnah.
Their proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas without a command.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Explain why Hanafis give preference to Ibn Mas'ud: the presence of an explicit command outweighs the absence in the other hadith.
Refer to the usul principle that al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: Hanafi prayer recites 'al-tahiyyatu lillah...'; Shafi'i prayer recites 'al-tahiyyatu al-mubarakat...'
Also differences in the ruling of obligation vs. sunnah.

Commentary
This passage discusses two interconnected rulings: first, the obligation (wujub) of reciting tashahhud in the prayer, and second, the preferred wording according to the Hanafi school. The text argues that tashahhud is wajib based on the imperative 'falyaqul' in the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, and because the Prophet taught it like a Quranic surah (indicating emphasis). The Hanafis prefer Ibn Mas'ud's version because of the explicit command and the teaching method. The Shafi'i school prefers Ibn Abbas's version, which includes 'al-mubarakat'. The key difference is not on the obligation but on which wording is more virtuous. In practice, both are valid, but the Hanafi fatwa is on Ibn Mas'ud's version.

Key Terms
تشهد (tashahhud): The recitation of the testimony of faith during the sitting in prayer
امر (amr): The imperative verb form in the hadith 'falyaqul' which indicates obligation

Only Tashahhud in the First Sitting
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on first sitting recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in the first sitting, cite the primary hadith evidence, and compare it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
In the first sitting, only tashahhud is recited; the Prophet's swift rising proves no addition is made.

This passage addresses a specific element of salah: what is recited during the first sitting (qa'dah ula). While many Muslims are accustomed to reciting salawat after tashahhud in every sitting, the Hanafi school holds that this should only be done in the final sitting. The evidence comes from the practice of the Prophet himself, who rose so quickly from the first sitting that he seemed to be on hot stones. This lesson explores the ruling and the inter-madhhab difference with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of recitation during the first sitting. What is the Hanafi ruling? No addition beyond the tashahhud.
The first sitting occurs in Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, and Isha prayers after the second rak'ah.
2
Present the Hanafi Dalil
Locate the hadith cited: from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud describing the Prophet's swift rising. Understand how this proves the ruling.
The hadith is narrated by Nasa'i and Abu Dawud.
3
Present the Shafi'i Position and Dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that salawat should be recited in the first sitting based on general hadiths encouraging salawat after tashahhud.
These general hadiths include 'Whoever sends blessings upon me, Allah sends blessings upon him tenfold.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response: the specific action of the Prophet (quickly rising) indicates that the general recommendation does not apply in the first sitting, as the Prophet did not do it.
This is a classic case of takhsis al-amm (specificating the general) by the Prophet's practice.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
What is the practical difference? Hanafis recite only tashahhud and stand; Shafi'is recite tashahhud, then salawat, then stand.
This difference affects the actual performance of the prayer.

قعدہ اولی میں تشہد سے زیادہ نہ پڑھے۔ اتنا ہی پڑھ کر تیسری رکعت کے لئے کھڑا ہو جائے۔
عن عبد الله بن مسعود قال كان النبى الله في الركعتين كانه على الرضف قلت حتى يقوم قال ذلك يريد (الف) ( نسائی شریف ، باب التخفيف في التشهد الاول ، ص ۱۶۴، نمبر۷ ۱۷ار ابو داؤد شریف ، باب فی تخفیف القعود ص ۱۵۰ نمبر ۹۹۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ آپ قعدہ اولی میں اتنی جلدی اٹھتے تھے جیسے آپ گرم پتھر پر ہوں۔ اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ قعدہ اولی میں تشہد سے زیادہ نہ پڑھے۔
فا امام شافعی کے نزدیک عقدہ اولی میں بھی تشہد کے بعد درود پڑھے گا۔ ان کی دلیل وہ احادیث ہے جن میں تشہد کے بعد درود کی فضیلت آئی ہے۔
And he does not add to this in the first sitting. In the first sitting, do not recite more than tashahhud. Just recite this much and stand up for the third rak'ah. From Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: 'The Prophet in the two rak'ahs was as if on hot stones.' I said: 'Until he stands?' He said: 'That is what he intended.' This hadith indicates that the Prophet used to stand up from the first sitting so quickly, as if on hot stones. So one should not recite more than tashahhud in the first sitting. According to Imam Shafi'i, in the first sitting one should recite salawat after tashahhud. His evidence is the hadiths that mention the virtue of salawat after tashahhud.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific rule: in the first sitting (qa'dah ula) of a prayer that has two sittings (e.g., Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, Isha), one should recite only the tashahhud and then immediately stand for the next rak'ah. The Hanafi school prohibits adding salawat (durood) or any other remembrance in this sitting. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud describing the Prophet's swiftness in the first sitting, as if sitting on heated stones. Imam Shafi'i differs, maintaining that salawat should be recited in the first sitting as well, based on general hadiths encouraging salawat after tashahhud. The Hanafi response implicitly is that the specific practice of the Prophet (quick rising) takes precedence over general virtue hadiths. The practical outcome: Hanafis do not recite salawat in the first sitting; Shafi'is do.

Key Terms
قعدة أولى (qa'dah ula): The first sitting in prayers that have two sittings (Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, Isha)
رضف (radhf): Hot stones; used in the hadith to describe the Prophet's quickness in the first sitting

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding recitation in the first sitting (qa'dah ula)?
A) Recite tashahhud followed by salawat
B) Recite only the tashahhud and stand
C) Recite tashahhud and then a supplication
D) Recite salawat only

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's practice in the first sitting is to recite only tashahhud and stand quickly; therefore, Hanafis do not add salawat.

Recitation in the Last Two Rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr
Rules what is to be recited in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr: Fatiha is better, but tasbih is also acceptable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr and explain the evidence from the hadith of Abu Qatada.

Core Idea
Recitation of Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs is recommended, not obligatory; tasbih suffices.

This lesson addresses a common point of confusion: the ruling on recitation in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the first and last rak'ahs, permitting lighter recitation or even glorifications. We will examine the primary evidence from the hadith of Abu Qatada and the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is recitation in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr. The ruling: it is not fard; reciting Fatiha is better (afdal), but tasbih is also acceptable.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith of Abu Qatada: 'The Prophet used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr Fatiha and two surahs, and in the last two rak'ahs Fatiha.'
This is a sahih hadith, but zanni in transmission.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's practice (fi'l) of reciting only Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs indicates that additional recitation is not obligatory. The absence of a second surah suggests leniency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that Fatiha is fard in every rak'ah, including the last two. Their evidence is general commands to recite in prayer.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Hanafi scholars prioritize the explicit practice of the Prophet and the understanding of the Companions, as seen in Umar's approval of Sa'd's practice of shortening the last two rak'ahs.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۷] (۳۷) ويقرأ في الركعتين الاخيرتين بفاتحة
ان ظہر اور عصر کی دوسری دو رکعتوں میں قرآت فرض نہیں ہے اس لئے سورۂ فاتحہ پڑھے تو یہ بہتر ہے۔ اور تسبیح پڑھے وہ بھی ٹھیک ہے۔
عن عبد الله بن ابي قتادة عن ابيه ان النبي علا الله كان يقرأ في الظهر في الأوليين بام الكتاب وسورتين و في الركعتين الاخريين بام الكتاب ويسمعنا الآية ويطول في الركعة الأولى ما لا يطيل في الركعة الثانية وهكذا في العصر (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب قر آفی الآخرين بفاتحه الکتاب ص ۰۷ نمبر ۶ے سے مسلم شریف ، باب القرآة في الظهر والعصر ص ۱۸۵ نمبر ۴۵۱)
(۲) جابر بن سمرة قال قال عمر ا لسعد لقد شكوك في كل شيء حتى الصلوة قال اما انا فامد في الاوليين واحذف في الآخرين ولا آتو ما اقتديت به من صلوة رسول الله قال صدقت ذاك الظن بک (ج) ( بخاری شریف
And he recites in the last two rak'ahs [only] Fatiha. In Zuhr and Asr, recitation is not fard in the last two rak'ahs, so if he recites Fatiha it is better, and if he recites tasbih it is also acceptable. From Abdullah ibn Abi Qatada from his father that the Prophet used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr Fatiha and two surahs, and in the last two rak'ahs Fatiha, and he would make us hear the verse occasionally, and he would prolong the first rak'ah more than the second, and similarly in Asr. And from Jabir ibn Samura: Umar said to Sa'd: 'People have complained about you in everything, even prayer.' He said: 'I prolong the first two and shorten the last two, and I do not fall short of what I follow from the prayer of the Messenger of Allah.' Umar said: 'You have spoken the truth, that is what I thought of you.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses what to recite in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr. In the Hanafi school, recitation of the Quran is not obligatory (fard) in these rak'ahs; it is optional. The better practice is to recite Surah al-Fatiha, but one may also recite tasbih (e.g., subhanallah) or remain silent. The author provides two proofs: the hadith of Abu Qatada describes the Prophet's practice of reciting Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs, and the incident between Umar and Sa'd shows that the Salaf understood that the last two rak'ahs are shorter and do not require additional surahs. Note that the Shafi'i school may differ (they consider Fatiha obligatory in every rak'ah), but this is not explicitly disputed here. The Hanafi view is based on the fact that the Prophet's practice was consistent and the Companions understood that recitation is not fard in the last two.

Key Terms
فاتحة (fatiha): Surah al-Fatiha, the opening chapter of the Quran
تسبيح (tasbih): Recitation of 'subhanallah' or similar glorifications as a substitute for Quranic recitation

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is better (afdal) but not obligatory
C) It is forbidden (haram)
D) It is the same as in the first two rak'ahs

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, recitation in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr is not obligatory; Fatiha is recommended, but tasbih suffices.

No Addition Beyond Tashahhud in First Sitting
Ruling prohibiting extra recitation beyond tashahhud in first sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the first sitting, identify the evidence from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, and explain the difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Only tashahhud is recited in the first sitting; any addition is makruh tahrimi.

This lesson examines the specific ruling regarding the first sitting (qa'dah ula) in prayer. While many are aware that tashahhud is recited, there is a difference among schools whether additional blessings should be included. We will explore the Hanafi position, its proof, and the practical consequence of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the first sitting in a prayer of three or four rak'ahs. The ruling is that only the tashahhud is recited, and nothing more should be added.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud describing the Prophet's haste in the first sitting, as recorded by Nasa'i and Abu Dawood.
The hadith text: 'كان النبي في الركعتين كأنه على الرضف'
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The description of being 'as if on hot stones' indicates extreme brevity, implying that only the necessary tashahhud was recited and no prolonging with extra du'a or blessings.
4
Compare with opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that sending blessings upon the Prophet after tashahhud is recommended even in the first sitting, based on hadiths extolling the virtue of salat after tashahhud.
5
Determine the practical difference
In Hanafi practice, the worshiper stands immediately after tashahhud; in Shafi'i practice, one recites blessings before standing.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

[207] And recite in the last two rak'ahs with the Opening. Tashahhud is sunnah. Reciting tashahhud is wajib, whichever tashahhud it may be, because in the above hadith there is the form of a command which establishes obligation. The author also notes the Shafi'i position that blessings (salat) upon the Prophet should be recited in the first sitting as well, supported by hadiths on the virtue of such blessings. The Hanafi ruling, based on the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, is that one should not add anything beyond the tashahhud.

Key Terms

تشهد (tashahhud): The declaration of faith recited in the sitting position of prayer: 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi...'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly limits the first sitting to tashahhud, following the Prophet's example of brevity, and considers any addition as makruh tahrimi.

8 min
Recitation in the Last Two Rak'ahs: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Ruling on reciting only Surah al-Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs, with Hanafi and Shafi'i views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in the last two rak'ahs and explain the evidence from the hadith of Abu Qatada.

Core Idea
Reciting Surah al-Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs is mustahabb, not fard — the Prophet's practice proves it.

In the four-rak'ah prayers, the recitation requirement differs between the first two and the last two rak'ahs. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that recitation in the last two rak'ahs is not obligatory, supported by the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the companion Sa'd. We will also explore the internal Hanafi variation and the Shafi'i counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the prayer
The act is recitation (qira'ah) in the last two rak'ahs of four-rak'ah prayers (Dhuhr, Asr, Isha) and the last rak'ah of Maghrib (though often grouped as 'last two').
The lesson focuses on Dhuhr and Asr as examples.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Recitation in the last two rak'ahs is not fard. The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that reciting Surah al-Fatihah is mustahabb (preferable), and one may also recite tasbih (subhanallah, etc.).
Some Hanafi narrations consider it wajib, but the majority view is mustahabb.
3
Examine the primary dalil
The hadith of Abu Qatada (ra) reports that the Prophet (ﷺ) recited only Umm al-Kitab (al-Fatihah) in the last two rak'ahs of Dhuhr and Asr.
This hadith is in Bukhari and Muslim, indicating it is sahih.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, some early narrations (e.g., from Imam Muhammad) consider reciting Fatihah as wajib, but the later relied-upon view is mustahabb due to the strength of the practice evidence.
The commentary leans toward mustahabb.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that reciting Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs is fard, citing the hadith 'No prayer without the Fatihah of the Book' and the same hadith of Abu Qatada to argue that the Prophet recited it, thus it is required.
The practical consequence: in Shafi'i school, missing Fatihah invalidates the rak'ah; in Hanafi, it is permissible to substitute with tasbih.

'Umar said: 'You have spoken the truth; that is what I thought of you.' (Bukhari) Footnotes: (a) The Prophet was in the first tashahhud as if on hot stones. I said, 'Until he stands?' He said, 'That is what he meant.' (b) The Prophet used to recite in Dhuhr in the first two rak'ahs Fatihah and two surahs, and in the last two rak'ahs Fatihah, and he would make us hear the verse sometimes, and he would lengthen the first rak'ah more than the second, and similarly in Asr. (c) 'Umar said to Sa'd: 'People have complained about you in everything, even in prayer.' Sa'd said: 'I lengthen the first two rak'ahs and shorten the last two, and I do not fall short in following the prayer of the Messenger of Allah.' 'Umar said: 'You have spoken the truth; that is what I thought of you.' From the first hadith it is understood that reciting Surah al-Fatihah in the last rak'ah is sufficient. And the second hadith also indicates that he shortened in the last two rak'ahs, which may mean he only recited Surah al-Fatihah. Therefore, for the Hanafis, in some narrations reciting Surah al-Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs is wajib, and in some narrations it is mustahabb. As for Imam al-Shafi'i, he holds that reciting Surah al-Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs is fard, based on (1) the above hadith and (2) the hadith 'No prayer without the Fatihah of the Book' which has been referenced earlier.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage discusses the recitation in the last two rak'ahs of prayers like Dhuhr and Asr. The author presents the Hanafi view that recitation is not obligatory; rather, one may recite Surah al-Fatihah (which is preferable) or simply say tasbih. The evidence includes the practice of the Prophet who recited only Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs, and the practice of the Companions like Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas who shortened the last two rak'ahs. There is a difference of narration among Hanafi sources: some consider reciting Fatihah wajib, others mustahabb. The Shafi'i position holds it as fard based on hadiths. The Hanafi school allows flexibility in the last two rak'ahs.

Key Terms
الركعتين الاخيرتين (al-rak'atayn al-ākhiratayn): The last two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer (Dhuhr, Asr, Isha) or the last rak'ah of Maghrib. Here refers to the last two of four.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Reciting Surah al-Fatihah is mustahabb, and one may also recite tasbih.
C) It is forbidden to recite anything other than Fatihah.
D) Reciting a surah after Fatihah is mandatory.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that recitation in the last two rak'ahs is not obligatory; the preferred practice is to recite Surah al-Fatihah, but tasbih suffices.

The Final Sitting in Prayer
Ruling on sitting posture and tashahhud in final sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi posture for the final sitting and identify the ruling on tashahhud.

Core Idea
The last sitting is identical to the first: sit on left foot, right foot upright, and recite tashahhud.

This lesson clarifies the posture for the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) according to the Hanafi school. The text emphasizes that the posture is the same as the first sitting (iftirash) and explicitly prohibits tawarruk. Understanding this ruling is essential for correct prayer performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sitting positions described
The passage describes two sitting postures: (1) first sitting (qa'dah ula) – sitting on the left foot, right foot upright; (2) last sitting (qa'dah akhirah) – same as the first sitting.
Tawarruk is specifically prohibited in the last sitting.
2
Understand the ruling on posture
The ruling is that the last sitting must be in iftirash (sitting on left foot, right foot upright). Tawarruk is not allowed.
This is a practical rule; deviating may affect the prayer's completeness.
3
Recognize the obligation of tashahhud
The passage states that tashahhud is recited in the last sitting. The proof is referenced from an earlier discussion.
Tashahhud is a wajib (obligatory) recitation in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Connect to the broader context
The ruling is part of the detailed sunnah/posture rules in salah. Consistency between sittings is emphasized.

Source Text

Key Terms
قعدة أخيرة (qa'dah akhīrah): The final sitting in prayer after completing the last rak'ah, before the salams.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the left hip with both feet protruding to the right side.

Sending Blessings upon the Prophet in the Last Sitting: Sunnah, Not Wajib
Ruling and proofs for reciting salat upon the Prophet in the last sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sending blessings upon the Prophet in the last sitting, identify the Quranic and hadith proofs, and explain why omitting it does not invalidate the prayer.

Core Idea
Sending blessings on the Prophet in the last sitting is sunnah; its omission neither invalidates prayer nor requires sajdah sahw.

The act of sending blessings (salat) upon the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ during the final sitting of prayer is a meritorious practice. But is it obligatory? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi stance: it is a recommended sunnah, not a wajib or fard. We will examine the textual evidence drawn from the Quran and Sunnah and understand how the Hanafi school derives this lenient ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Recognize that the Hanafi position categorizes sending blessings as sunnah, not wajib. Note the consequence: no sajdah sahw for omission.
2
Analyze the Quranic Proof
Examine the command in Surah al-Ahzab 33:56 and understand the Hanafi interpretation that the imperative applies to once in a lifetime, not specifically to prayer.
The imperative 'sallu' indicates a command, but the Hanafis argue the Quran does not specify a location, so it is fulfilled once in a lifetime.
3
Examine the Hadith Proofs
Look at the hadith of Ka'b ibn 'Ujra teaching the formula and the hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd warning about incomplete prayer. Understand how these proofs support the sunnah ruling.
The hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd is reported by Darqutni and is not of the highest authenticity, contributing to the sunnah classification.
4
Contrast with Other Schools
Briefly note that the Shafi'i school considers it wajib, highlighting the difference in usul methodology.
Shafi'is rely on a stronger interpretation of the hadith 'there is no prayer for one who does not...' as wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۹] (۳۹) اور حضور پر درود پڑھے۔ ا قعدہ اخیرہ میں تشہد کے بعد حضور پر درد پڑھے۔ درود پڑھنا سنت ہے اس کو پڑھنا چاہئے لیکن اگر نہیں پڑھے گا تب بھی سجدہ سہو لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ نماز میں درودسنت ہونے کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے ان الله و ملئكته يصلون على النبي يا ايها الذين آمنوا صلوا عليه وسلموا تسليما (الف) آیت ۵۶ سورۃ الاحزاب ۳۳) اس آیت سے زندگی میں ایک مرتبہ درود پڑھنا فرض ہے اور نماز میں پڑھنا سنت ہے(۲) حدیث میں ہے عن كعب بن عجرة ... فقلنا قد عرفنا کیف نسلم علیک فکیف نصلی علیک؟ قال قولوا اللهم صلی علی محمد الخ (ب) ( مسلم شریف، باب الصلوۃ علی النبی بعد التشهد ص ۷۵ نمبر ۴۰۶ را بو داؤد شریف ، باب الصلو علی النبی بعد التشهد ص ۱۴۷ نمبر ۹۷۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ تشہد کے بعد حضور پر درود پڑھنا چاہیے (۳) عن سهل بن سعد ان النبي الله قال لا صلوة لمن يصل على نبيه الا الله (ج) (دار قطنی ، باب ذکر وجوب الصلوة على النبي في التشهد ص ۳۴۷ نمبر ۱۳۲۷) اس حدیث سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ حضور پر درود پڑھنا چاہئے ۔
[209] (39) And he should send blessings upon the Prophet (peace be upon him). In the last sitting, after tashahhud, he should send blessings upon the Prophet. Sending blessings is sunnah; one should do it, but if he does not, then sajdah sahw is not necessary. The proof that sending blessings in prayer is sunnah is this verse: 'Indeed, Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet. O you who have believed, send blessings upon him and salute him with a greeting of peace' (Surah al-Ahzab, 33:56). From this verse, sending blessings once in a lifetime is fard, and in prayer it is sunnah. (2) There is a hadith from Ka'b ibn 'Ujra... We said: 'We know how to greet you, but how do we send blessings upon you?' He said: 'Say: O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad...' (Muslim, chapter on sending blessings upon the Prophet after tashahhud, p. 75 no. 406; Abu Dawood, chapter on blessing the Prophet after tashahhud, p. 147 no. 976). From this hadith it is understood that one should send blessings upon the Prophet after tashahhud. (3) From Sahl ibn Sa'd that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no prayer for one who does not send blessings upon his Prophet.' (Darqutni, chapter on mentioning the obligation of sending blessings upon the Prophet in the tashahhud, p. 347 no. 1327). This hadith also indicates that one should send blessings upon the Prophet.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and multiple proofs for sending blessings (salat) upon the Prophet in the last sitting of prayer. The author clarifies that it is a sunnah, not an obligation; if omitted, no sajdah sahw is required. The proofs include the Quranic command which is interpreted as making it fard once in a lifetime, but in prayer it is sunnah. Two hadiths are cited: one teaching the formula of sending blessings, and another emphasizing its importance. The author thus establishes the recommended but non-obligatory nature of this act in prayer according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
درود (durūd): Blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, specifically the formula 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad...'
سجدہ سہو (sajdah al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness performed to compensate for unintentional changes in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is wajib (necessary).
C) It is sunnah (recommended).
D) It is makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
Sending blessings on the Prophet in the last sitting is a sunnah act; its omission does not affect the validity of the prayer.

Supplication in the Last Sitting: Permissible Du'a after Tashahhud
Ruling on permissible supplications in the last sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for permissible supplication in the last sitting according to the Hanafi school and identify which phrases are allowed.

Core Idea
Du'a after tashahhud must be Quranic or prophetic in style, not resembling ordinary speech.

After completing the tashahhud in the last sitting, a worshipper may engage in supplication. However, the Hanafi school places a restriction: the words used must be in the style of the Quran or hadith, avoiding phrases that are merely human speech. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is du'a (supplication) after tashahhud in the last sitting of salah. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) with conditions.
2
Extract the conditions from the text
The text states two conditions: (1) the du'a must resemble words of the Quran and supplications transmitted in hadith; (2) it must not resemble ordinary human speech (kalam al-nas).
3
Understand the prohibition
The prohibition is against using phrases that are typical of everyday conversation, even if in Arabic, as they detract from the reverence of prayer.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
For instance, 'Allahumma ighfir li' (a prophetic du'a) is allowed, but 'Give me a car' in one's own words is not.
5
Note the underlying principle
The principle is preserving the dignity of prayer (hurmat al-salah) by avoiding worldly talk within it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۰] (۴۰) اور دعا کرے جو چاہے ایسی دعا جو الفاظ قرآن کے مشابہ ہواور حدیث میں منقول دعا ئیں ہوں اور نہ دعا کرے ایسی جو کلام الناس کے مشابہ ہو۔
[210] (40) And he may make any supplication he wishes, provided it resembles words of the Quran and supplications transmitted in hadith, and he should not make supplication that resembles human speech.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on supplication (du'a) in the last sitting after tashahhud. The author allows the worshipper to make any supplication, but with conditions: it should be in the style of Quranic phrases or hadith supplications, avoiding phrases that are ordinary human speech. This ensures the prayer remains dignified and distinct from worldly talk.

Key Terms
دعا (du'ā): Supplication to Allah made during prayer, particularly after tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Loud Recitation in Fajr, Maghrib and Isha
Ruling with proof for jahr in specific prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on jahr in specific prayers and identify the hadith evidence for each case.

Core Idea
Jahr in Fajr and first two rak'ahs of Maghrib/Isha is sunnah mu'akkadah for the imam, not fard.

How does a Hanafi imam lead prayer in terms of recitation volume? This passage clarifies the rulings and provides direct hadith evidence for loud recitation in certain prayers and silent recitation in others. It also addresses a related issue about the necessity of salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is loud recitation (jahr) by the imam in specific prayer units. The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah) for Fajr (both rak'ahs) and the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha.
Jahr is not fard or wajib; its omission without reason is makruh.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The author cites multiple hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: for Fajr, the hadith of jinn listening; for Maghrib, Jubair bin Mut'im hearing Surah Tur; for Isha, Bara' hearing Surah Tin. Also, a hadith from Sa'd is used for silent recitation in the last two rak'ahs.
All hadith are ahad (single-transmitter), so they provide zanni (speculative) proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (indication)
The key word is 'سمعت' (I heard) in the hadith, which indicates that the recitation was audible. This establishes that the Prophet (peace be upon him) recited aloud in these prayers, and thus the imam should follow suit.
The word 'سمعت' implies direct hearing, so the recitation must have been loud enough to be heard.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
The passage notes that Imam al-Shafi'i considers salam (taslim) as fard based on a different hadith. This is a separate issue, but it shows that the Hanafi school does not consider salam as a pillar; rather, the prayer is complete with the final sitting (qa'dah).
The disagreement on salam does not affect the jahr ruling, but it illustrates different methodologies in evaluating hadith.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preference)
The Hanafi position relies on multiple authentic hadith directly describing the Prophet's practice. The ruling is well-established in the zahir al-riwayah and is the mufta bihi position. The evidence for jahr is clear and not overridden by other considerations.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah; the position is consistent across the Hanafi school.

And he recites aloud in Fajr and in the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha... (Urdu commentary: From this hadith it is understood that if one sits in the final sitting and hadath occurs, the prayer is complete, whether he says salam or not. So salam is not fard. If salam were fard, how would the prayer be complete? From Ibn Umar: the Prophet said, 'When the imam completes the prayer and sits, then has hadath before speaking, his prayer is complete, and those behind him who have completed the prayer are also complete.' (Abu Dawud). So salam is not fard. According to Imam Shafi'i, the tahlila for salam makes it fard. (212)(42): He recites aloud in Fajr, and in the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha if he is imam. And he recites silently after the first two. In Fajr both rak'ahs, in the first two of Maghrib and Isha, he recites aloud. In the third of Maghrib and the last two of Isha, he recites silently. Hadith from Ibn Abbas: The Prophet went out and was praying Fajr with his companions, and when the jinn heard the Quran they listened attentively (Bukhari, Muslim). In this chapter, several hadiths are mentioned about jahr in Fajr in front of jinn, indicating that Fajr prayer has jahr if one is imam. The proof for jahr in Maghrib is from Jubair bin Mut'im from his father: I heard the Prophet recite Surah Tur in Maghrib (Bukhari). The word 'sam'tu' indicates he recited aloud. The proof for jahr in Isha is the hadith from Bara': The Prophet was on a journey and recited in Isha in one of the two rak'ahs 'At-Teen wa az-Zaytun' (Bukhari, Muslim). Also sam'tu indicates jahr. The proof for not reciting aloud in the last two rak'ahs of Isha is the hadith: Umar said to Sa'd, 'You are doubted in everything, even prayer.' Sa'd said, 'As for me, I elongate in the first two and shorten in the last two.' (Bukhari)
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof for loud recitation (jahr) in specific prayers. The author states that the imam recites aloud in Fajr (both rak'ahs) and in the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha. He recites silently after the first two. The Urdu commentary clarifies that the final sitting (qad'a) completes the prayer, and salam is not fard; this is supported by a hadith from Ibn Umar. It notes that Imam Shafi'i considers salam fard based on another hadith. The passage then provides multiple hadith proofs for jahr in each prayer: for Fajr, the hadith of jinn listening; for Maghrib, the hadith of Jubair bin Mut'im hearing Surah Tur; for Isha, the hadith of Bara' hearing Surah Tin; and for silence in the last two, the practice of Sa'd reported by Umar. These establish the Hanafi position that jahr is sunnah mu'akkadah for the imam in these prayers, while silence is observed in Zuhr, Asr, and the last rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Loud recitation that can be heard by others.
الإسرار (al-israr): Silent recitation, audible only to oneself.
التسليم (al-taslim): The final salam (saying 'As-salamu alaykum') to end the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both rak'ahs of Fajr and all rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha
B) Both rak'ahs of Fajr, first two of Maghrib and first two of Isha
C) Only in Fajr and Maghrib
D) Only in the first two rak'ahs of every prayer

Key Takeaway
Jahr in Fajr and first two of Maghrib/Isha is sunnah mu'akkadah for the imam, supported by explicit hadith; salam is not fard but wajib.

The Munfarid’s Recitation: Choosing Between Loud and Silent
Ruling on the munfarid's choice with proof from athar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a munfarid regarding recitation volume and explain the proof from the practice of Abdullah ibn Umar.

Core Idea
A munfarid may choose to recite aloud or silently; neither is obligatory.

This lesson clarifies the flexibility given to a person praying alone. In the Hanafi school, the munfarid is like an imam for himself, but since no one is behind him, he may choose between loud and silent recitation. This ruling is supported by the practice of the Companion Abdullah ibn Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and subject
The act is the recitation volume in prayer for a person praying alone (munfarid).
2
State the ruling
The munfarid has a choice: he may recite aloud or silently. Neither is obligatory.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is an athar from Abdullah ibn Umar, reported in Muwatta Imam Malik.
The athar shows Ibn Umar reciting aloud when making up missed rak'ahs alone.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
Ibn Umar's practice indicates that a munfarid may choose jahr, as he recited aloud when alone.
5
Note the recommended practice
Although permissible, it is better to recite somewhat aloud to emulate the congregational form.

And if he is praying alone, he has a choice: if he wishes, he recites aloud and makes himself hear; if he wishes, he recites silently. [213] (43) And if he is praying alone, he has a choice: if he wishes, he recites aloud and makes himself hear; if he wishes, he recites silently. Explanation: If he is praying alone, he is also an imam for himself, so in a prayer where jahr is prescribed, he can recite aloud and make himself hear. But there is no one behind him to hear, so he can also recite silently. It is better to recite somewhat aloud so that the prayer is performed in the manner of congregation. The proof is the athar from Nafi' that Abdullah ibn Umar, when he missed something of the prayer with the imam in a prayer where the imam recited aloud, when the imam said salam, Abdullah would stand and recite for himself in what he was making up, and he would recite aloud. (Muwatta Imam Malik). From this it is understood that if one prays a prayer that is normally recited aloud, he can recite aloud, because Abdullah ibn Umar used to recite aloud.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof for the munfarid's choice. The author states that a person praying alone may recite either aloud or silently. The Urdu commentary explains that the munfarid is like an imam for himself, so he may recite aloud in prayers where jahr is typical, but since no one is behind him, silence is also permissible. It is recommended to recite audibly to resemble the congregational form. The proof is from the practice of Abdullah ibn Umar, who when making up missed rak'ahs after the imam, would recite aloud. This shows that a munfarid may choose jahr.

Key Terms
منفرد (munfarid): A person praying alone, not in congregation.
جهر (jahr): Loud recitation in prayer, audible to others.
إسرار (israr): Silent recitation, only audible to oneself.

Key Takeaway
A munfarid may freely choose between loud and silent recitation; the better practice is to recite aloud to maintain the form of congregational prayer.

The Imam Conceals Recitation in the Latter Rak'ahs
Ruling and commentary on imam's silent recitation in last rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the imam's recitation in the third and fourth rak'ahs and explain the basis for silent recitation.

Core Idea
The imam must recite silently in the latter rak'ahs; silent recitation is the established practice, not complete omission.

This passage establishes a key ruling for the imam's recitation in the latter units of prayer. Based on a hadith that indicates the Prophet (ﷺ) did not recite aloud, the Hanafi school rules that the imam conceals his recitation. The commentary clarifies that the preferred view is silent recitation rather than complete omission, and by analogy the same applies to the third rak'ah of Maghrib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The ruling states that if a person is leading the prayer as an imam, he conceals his recitation (yakhfi al-qira'ah) in the rak'ahs after the first two. This applies to the third and fourth rak'ahs of four-rak'ah prayers (Zuhr, Asr, Isha) and the third rak'ah of Maghrib.
The term 'ba'd al-ulayyayn' means after the first two.
2
Understand the basis from the commentary
The commentary explains that the hadith indicates two possibilities: the Prophet (ﷺ) either omitted recitation entirely (hadhf) or recited softly. The author of the text prefers the interpretation that he recited softly, meaning the imam does recite but silently.
The word 'hadhf' strictly means omission, but the author argues the silent recitation is more consistent with the overall practice.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas) to Maghrib
The third rak'ah of Maghrib is analogized to the latter two rak'ahs of four-rak'ah prayers. Therefore, the imam recites silently in the third rak'ah of Maghrib as well.
Maghrib has three rak'ahs; the first two are recited aloud, the third silently.
4
Distinguish between the imam and the individual
This ruling specifically applies to the imam. For the person praying alone, there is a choice to recite aloud or silently in all rak'ahs, though silent is preferred. The imam's role requires following the sunnah of jahr and ikhfa.
The text mentions that the individual's ruling will be explained later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان اماما ويخفى القراءة فيما بعد الاوليين اس حديث سے معلوم ہوا کہ دوسری دور کعتوں میں قرآت نہ کرتے تھے یا آہستہ کرتے تھے ۔ کیونکہ حذف کے ایک ہی معنی ہے قرآت چھوڑ دینا ۔ اس لئے قرآت ہی نہیں کرتے تھے یا آہستہ کرتے تھے۔ اسی پر مغرب کی تیسری رکعت کو قیاس کرنا چاہئے۔ اور اس میں بھی یا قرآت نہیں ہے جیسا کہ پہلے گزرا یا قرآت آہستہ کرتے تھے۔
If he is an imam, he conceals recitation in the (rak'ahs) after the first two. From this hadith it is known that in the second two rak'ahs they did not recite or they recited softly. Because the word 'hadhf' (omission) has only one meaning: leaving out recitation. Therefore they either did not recite at all or recited softly. The third rak'ah of Maghrib should be analogized to this. And in it also either there is no recitation as has passed, or they recited softly.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its commentary. The ruling states that if a person is leading the prayer as an imam, he conceals his recitation in the rak'ahs after the first two (i.e., the third and fourth rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer such as Zuhr, Asr, and Isha, and similarly the third rak'ah of Maghrib). The commentary explains that this is based on a hadith which indicates that the Prophet (ﷺ) did not recite aloud in these rak'ahs, but either omitted recitation entirely or recited softly. The author prefers the interpretation that recitation is performed softly, not omitted. By analogy (qiyas), the third rak'ah of Maghrib is treated the same as the latter two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer. This is the Hanafi position: the imam recites silently (khafd) in the latter rak'ahs, and the individual has a choice as will be explained later.

Key Terms
يخفى القراءة (yakhfi al-qira'ah): conceals recitation, meaning recites silently
بعد الاوليين (ba'd al-ulayyayn): after the first two, i.e., the third and fourth rak'ahs
حذف (hadhf): omission, here referring to the possibility that recitation is completely omitted

Key Takeaway
In the latter rak'ahs, the imam recites silently; this is based on prophetic practice and analogy.

The Individual's Choice in Recitation During Prayer
Ruling and commentary on the munfarid's option to recite aloud or silently

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person praying alone (munfarid) regarding audible recitation and identify the recommended practice.

Core Idea
An individual praying alone may recite aloud or silently; it is better to recite aloud to resemble congregation.

When you pray alone, do you have to recite silently? The Hanafi school gives you a choice. This lesson examines the ruling for the munfarid (one praying alone) in prayers where the imam normally recites aloud (Fajr, Maghrib, Isha). We will explore the textual basis and the preferred practice, drawing on the action of the Companion 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage concerns the munfarid (individual praying alone) in prayers that are normally recited aloud (jahr prayers: Fajr, Maghrib, Isha). The ruling is that he has a choice (mubah) between reciting aloud or silently.
This does not apply to silent prayers (Zuhr, Asr), where the munfarid must recite silently.
2
Understand the recommended practice
Although the act is permissible either way, the commentary states that it is better (afdal) to recite somewhat loudly so that the prayer resembles congregational prayer in form.
The recommendation is slight audibility, not full loudness.
3
Examine the proof
The proof is the practice (athar) of 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar (ra). When he missed a rak'ah behind an imam reciting aloud, he would stand after the imam's salam and recite aloud for himself. This indicates permissibility for the munfarid to recite aloud.
The proof is a qawl sahabi (saying of a Companion) and is zanni (speculative) in both transmission and indication.
4
Recognize the scope and limitations
The choice applies only to jahr prayers. For sirr prayers (Zuhr, Asr), the munfarid must recite silently. Also, the ruling does not apply to the muqtadi (follower) in congregation, who remains silent in the Hanafi school.
The text implies jahr prayers; this is confirmed by the context of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۳) وان كان منفردا فهو مخير ان شاء جهر واسمع نفسه وان شاء خافت تشریح اگر تنہا نماز پڑھ رہا ہے تو وہ اپنے لئے امام بھی ہے اس لئے جہری نماز میں زور سے پڑھ سکتا ہے اور اپنے آپ کو سنا سکتا ہے۔ لیکن اس کے پیچھے کوئی نہیں ہے جس کو سنائے اس لئے وہ آہستہ بھی پڑھ سکتا ہے۔ بہتر یہ ہے کہ تھوڑا زور سے پڑھے تا کہ ہیئت جماعت پر نماز ادا ہو اس کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے عن نافع ان عبد الله بن عمر كان اذا فاته شيء من الصلوة مع الامام فيما جهر فيه الامام بالقراءة انه اذا سلم الامام قام عبد الله فقرء لنفسه فيما يقضى و جهر (موطا امام مالک، باب العمل في القراءۃ ص ۶۴ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ جہری نماز کیلئے پڑھتا ہو تو زور سے قرآت پڑھ سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ عبداللہ بن عمر زور سے پڑھتے تھے۔ حاشیہ : (الف) عبد اللہ بن عمرؓ کا معاملہ یہ تھا کہ جب ان سے کوئی رکعت! ام کی نماز میں سے چھوٹ جاتی تو جس میں امام جہری قرآت کرتے ہوں تو جب امام سلام کرتے تو عبد اللہ بن عمر کھڑے ہوتے اور اپنے لئے پڑھتے وہ نماز جس کی قضا کرتے اور قرآت زور سے پڑھتے (یعنی فوت شدہ رکعت میں جہری قرآت فرماتے
(43) And if he is praying individually, he has a choice: if he wishes, he recites aloud and makes himself hear; and if he wishes, he recites silently. Explanation: If he is praying alone, he is also an imam for himself, therefore in audible prayers he can recite aloud and make himself hear. But there is no one behind him to hear, so he may also recite silently. It is better to recite somewhat loudly so that the prayer is performed in the manner of congregational prayer. Its proof is this athar from Nafi' that 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, when he missed something of the prayer with the imam in which the imam recited aloud, when the imam said salam, 'Abdullah would stand and recite for himself in what he was making up, and he recited aloud. (Muwatta' Malik, chapter on recitation). From this athar it is known that when one prays an audible prayer, he may recite aloud because 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar used to recite aloud. Footnote: The affair of 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar was that when he missed any rak'ah of the imam's prayer in which the imam recited aloud, when the imam said salam, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar would stand and recite for himself the prayer he was making up, and he recited aloud (i.e., in the missed rak'ah he would recite aloud).
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage contains the ruling (43) regarding the person praying alone (munfarid). The matn states that such a person has the choice to recite aloud (jahr) or silently (khafd) in prayers where the imam normally recites aloud (i.e., Fajr, Maghrib, and Isha). The commentary explains that since there is no congregation, he is effectively his own imam, so he may recite aloud. However, because there is no one to hear, he may also recite silently. The preferred (better) option is to recite somewhat loudly to emulate the form of congregational prayer. The proof cited is the practice of 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar (ra), who when making up missed rak'ahs behind the imam, would recite aloud. This indicates that an individual may recite aloud in audible prayers. The footnote clarifies that ibn 'Umar would recite aloud in the missed rak'ah even when praying alone. Thus, the Hanafi school gives the individual this choice.

Key Terms
منفردا (munfaridan): an individual praying alone
جهر (jahr): audible recitation
خافت (khafd): silent recitation

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Recite aloud loudly
D) Not recite at all

Key Takeaway
A munfarid has the choice to recite aloud or silently in audible prayers, with a preference for slight audibility to emulate the congregation.

Silent Recitation (Sariyyah) in Zuhr and Asr
Ruling and proof on silent recitation for imam in silent prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the imam must recite silently in Zuhr and Asr, identify the two hadith proofs, and explain that recitation in the latter two rak'ahs is not obligatory.

Core Idea
The imam must recite silently in Zuhr and Asr; occasional audible verses are exceptions.

This lesson presents ruling (44) of the text: the imam conceals his recitation in Zuhr and Asr. The proof comes from the practice of the Prophet ﷺ, who usually recited silently in these prayers, as reported by Abu Qatada and al-Zuhri. We also learn that recitation is only obligatory in the first two rak'ahs, a point inferred from the hadith's wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core ruling: the imam recites silently (yukhfi) in Zuhr and Asr. Recognise that this is a wajib (obligatory) act, not merely sunnah.
2
Examine the First Hadith (Abu Qatada)
Read the hadith: the Prophet recited Fatihah and a surah in the first two rak'ahs and occasionally made the companions hear a verse. Focus on the phrase 'yusmi'una al-ayah ahyanan' – what does this imply about his normal practice?
The phrase implies that usually he did not make them hear, i.e., he recited silently.
3
Analyse the Derivation
From 'he would recite in the two rak'ahs', understand that recitation is only prescribed in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr. The latter two rak'ahs do not require recitation (it is sunnah to recite silently, but not obligatory).
This is a key point: the hadith limits obligatory recitation to the first two rak'ahs.
4
Examine the Second Hadith (Al-Zuhri)
Read the mursal hadith of al-Zuhri which explicitly states: loud in Fajr and the first two of Maghrib and Isha, silent in all other prayers. This confirms the ruling for Zuhr and Asr.
This hadith provides a clear categorisation that includes Zuhr and Asr as silent prayers.
5
Synthesise the Evidence
Combine both hadith to establish that silent recitation is wajib for the imam in Zuhr and Asr. Note that occasional audible recitation (e.g., a verse) is permissible but does not change the default ruling.
If the imam intentionally recites aloud, he must compensate with sajdah sahw.
